Maybe the only fixed points are those where the three largest proper divisors are exactly N/2, N/3, N/6. That condition is equivalent to: N has at least three proper divisors, and the set of divisors greater than N/6 is exactly {N/2, N/3} (and possibly N itself, but not proper). So we need to characterize all N such that:

1. N is divisible by 6 (so N/2, N/3, N/6 are integers and proper divisors? Actually N/6 is a divisor; N/2 and N/3 are divisors).
2. For any divisor d of N with d > N/6, we have d = N/2 or d = N/3 (or d = N, but not proper).
Thus N/2 and N/3 must be the only divisors greater than N/6. Also note that N/6 itself is a divisor, and we need to ensure that there is no divisor d such that N/6 < d < N/3. That is the condition.

Now, what numbers satisfy that? Let's analyze the divisor structure.

Let N = 2^a 3^b M, with a,b≥1, gcd(M,6)=1. The divisors are 2^i3^j d, with i≤a, j≤b, d|M. The value is > N/6 = 2^{a-1}3^{b-1}M. So we need to consider all triples (i,j,d) such that 2^i3^j d > 2^{a-1}3^{b-1}M. Write the ratio: R = 2^{i-a+1}3^{j-b+1} d / M. Since d|M, d/M ≤ 1. Actually d/M is a rational number. But we can think in terms of the product. The divisors greater than N/6 are those with product > N/6. Let's find the condition for d to be a divisor greater than N/6. For each possible i,j,d, we have 2^i3^j d > 2^{a-1}3^{b-1}M.

Case 1: i=a-1, j=b-1. Then the condition becomes 2^{a-1}3^{b-1} d > 2^{a-1}3^{b-1} M => d > M. Since d|M, the only possibility is d=M? Actually d > M, but d|M implies d ≤ M. So d cannot be >M. Case 5: i=a, j=b-1? Already considered. i=a-1, j=b? Already considered. i=a, j=b? Already considered. Also consider i=a, j<b? Already covered by i=a, j=b-1? So the only divisors that can be > N/6 are those where either i≥a-1 and j≥b-1, or i=a and j=b-1, etc. But more systematically, we need to consider all triples (i,j,d) such that 2^i3^j d > 2^{a-1}3^{b-1}M. Divide both sides by 2^{a-1}3^{b-1}M: 2^{i-a+1}3^{j-b+1} d/M > 1. Since d|M, let M = d * t, where t is an integer (the co-divisor). Then the ratio is 2^{i-a+1}3^{j-b+1} t^{-1} > 1. Now note that i ranges 0..a, j 0..b, d|M. The divisors of N are parameterized by (i,j,d). The value is 2^i3^j d. We want to find all such divisors > N/6. That is, all triples satisfying 2^{i-a+1}3^{j-b+1} > M/d.

Now, for a given N, the set of divisors > N/6 will be those with product large. The largest divisors are typically those with i=a and j=b (N), then i=a,j=b-1 (N/3), then i=a-1,j=b (N/2), then i=a-1,j=b-1 (N/6), then possibly i=a,j=b-2? etc. But we need to check if any other divisor can exceed N/6. For the fixed point condition, we need that the only divisors > N/6 are N/3 and N/2 (and N itself, but not proper). So the largest proper divisor is N/2. So we need that the only divisors in (N/6, N] are N/2, N/3, and N. So we need to characterize N for which there is no divisor d such that N/6 < d < N/2, except possibly d = N/3. But note that N/3 is between N/6 and N/2 because 3<6? Actually N/3 > N/6 and N/3 < N/2? N/3 < N/2 iff 2<3, yes. So N/3 is in the interval. So we need that there is no divisor d with N/6 < d < N/3, and also no divisor d with N/3 < d < N/2? Actually N/2 is the largest, so any divisor between N/3 and N/2 would be a problem. So we need that the only divisors in (N/6, N/2) are N/3 and maybe N/2 itself? But N/2 is the endpoint. So the condition is: The set of divisors d with N/6 < d < N/2 is exactly {N/3} (or possibly empty if N/3 is not a divisor? But N/3 must be a divisor because N divisible by 3). So we need that there is no divisor between N/6 and N/3, and also no divisor between N/3 and N/2. But note that N/2 = 2 * N/6, and N/3 = 2 * N/6? Actually N/3 = 2 * N/6, yes. So the interval (N/6, N/3) is of the form (x,2x) with x=N/6. So we need no divisor in (x,2x) except possibly x itself and 2x? But 2x = N/3. So we need that the only divisors in [x,2x] are x and 2x. Similarly, the interval (2x,3x) where 3x=N/2? Actually N/2 = 3x, so interval (2x,3x) is (N/3, N/2). So we need no divisor in (2x,3x). So the condition is that the only divisors of N that are ≥ x are x, 2x, 3x (and N=6x). So we need that N has exactly three divisors in [x, N] (including endpoints), and those are x,2x,3x. That is equivalent to: N is of the form 2^a3^b M, and the divisors of N that are ≥ N/6 are exactly N/6, N/3, N/2. That is a strong condition.

Now, we have already considered cases with a=1,b=1 and M>1. For such N=6p (p prime >3), we have divisors: 1,2,3,6,p,2p,3p,6p. Here x=N/6=p. The divisors ≥ p are p,2p,3p,6p. So indeed only p,2p,3p are proper? 6p is N itself, not proper. So the proper divisors ≥ p are p,2p,3p. That satisfies condition. So N=6p with p prime ≥7 works.

What about N=2*3^b with b≥1? Then N=2*3^b, so x=N/6=3^{b-1}. Divisors: For b=2, N=18, divisors: 1,2,3,6,9,18. x=3. Proper divisors ≥3: 3,6,9? Actually 6 and 9 are both ≥3. 6 is 2x? 2x=6, 3x=9. Let's examine the mapping f(N) for numbers of the form 2^a * 3^b * M with M coprime to 6. We can try to compute f(N) in terms of a,b,M.

We already derived that for N with smallest prime factor 2, the three largest proper divisors are:

- d1 = N/2 = 2^{a-1}3^b M
- d2 = max( N/3, 2^{a-1}3^{b-1}M? Actually we need to consider the second largest. The divisors greater than N/6? Let's compute the top three proper divisors in general.

Let N = 2^a 3^b M, with M coprime to 6. The divisors are of the form 2^i 3^j d, where i=0..a, j=0..b, d|M. We want the three largest such numbers that are less than N. Since N itself is 2^a3^bM, the largest proper divisor is N/2 = 2^{a-1}3^bM. The second largest: It could be N/3 = 2^a3^{b-1}M, or it could be 2^{a-1}3^b (i.e., N/(2M)?) But N/(2M) = 2^{a-1}3^b, which is not necessarily a divisor? Actually 2^{a-1}3^b is a divisor because i=a-1,j=b,d=1. So it is a divisor. Compare N/3 = 2^a3^{b-1}M and N/(2M)=2^{a-1}3^b. Which is larger? Their ratio is (2^a3^{b-1}M)/(2^{a-1}3^b) = (2M)/3. So N/3 > N/(2M) if 2M > 3. Since M is at least 1, 2M≥2, so if M≥2, then 2M≥4>3, so N/3 > N/(2M). If M=1, then 2M=2<3, so N/3 < N/(2M). But careful: For M=1, N=2^a3^b. Then N/3 = 2^a3^{b-1}, and N/(2M)=2^{a-1}3^b. Which is larger? For a=1,b=2: N=18, N/3=6, N/(2M)=9, so N/(2M) is larger. So the ordering can change. So we need to consider the structure.

Similarly, the third largest proper divisor could be either N/6, or N/(2M), or something else.

Thus, to compute f(N) = d1+d2+d3, we need to determine the top three divisors. This is a combinatorial problem.

Maybe we can find a simpler characterization: The sequence a_n is defined by a_{n+1} = sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n. Perhaps we can prove that if a_n is even and divisible by 3, then a_{n+1} is divisible by 6? Let's test: 24→26, 26 is not divisible by 3? 26 mod3=2, so not divisible by 3. But 24 is divisible by 6? 24/6=4, yes divisible by 6. So 24→26, which is not divisible by 3. So that's not true.

Alternatively, note that the sum of three largest proper divisors is often close to N, but sometimes greater. Could it be that the only numbers that can appear in an infinite sequence are those that are multiples of 6 and also multiples of 3? Actually all multiples of 6 are multiples of 3.

Perhaps the problem has a known solution: The only possible a1 are those that are divisible by 6 and not divisible by 5? But we saw 12 is divisible by 6 and not by 5, fails. So that's not.

The third largest proper divisor is N/d_4. So f(N) = N/d_2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4. This is a neat formula. So we need to find the three smallest divisors >1 of N.

So the condition for a number N to be in the basin of attraction is that either N is a fixed point (f(N)=N) or it maps to a fixed point after some steps. So we need to find all N such that the forward orbit eventually lands on a fixed point. Since the fixed points are numbers with f(N)=N, we can try to characterize the set of numbers that map to fixed points.

Now, note that fixed points are numbers N such that the three largest proper divisors sum to N. Now, what about N = 72k? It gives f(N)=78k, fixed. So N works. Are there numbers that map to 78k? That would be numbers N such that f(N)=78k. Since 78k is fixed, we can find its preimages. That would be numbers N such that f(N)=72k. But 72k is not a fixed point generally. However, if 72k itself works, then it is in the basin. So we need to find all numbers that eventually land on a fixed point. This is a kind of "predecessor" problem. The set of all numbers that eventually reach a fixed point is exactly the set of numbers that are in the forward orbit of the fixed points under the inverse of f. Since f is not injective, the set of preimages of a fixed point can be quite large. For example, for a fixed point F, we can solve f(N)=F. This equation can have many solutions. We can try to characterize all N such that f(N) is a fixed point. This includes numbers of the form 72k (with k odd and not divisible by 5) and also numbers that are themselves fixed points (with 2||N and 5∤N). Also, there might be numbers that map to 72k, which then map to 78k. So we need to find all N such that iterating f eventually reaches a fixed point. This set might be exactly all numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5? But we saw 12 fails. So maybe the condition is that the number is divisible by 6 and also has the property that the sum of its three largest proper divisors is either equal to the number or eventually becomes a fixed point. This is a global condition.

Given the difficulty, perhaps there is a known result: For any integer N with at least three proper divisors, the sum of its three largest proper divisors is less than or equal to N, except for numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4? But we saw 30>30? Actually 30→31>30, so that's not.

Wait, maybe we can prove that if N is not a fixed point, then f(N) is either less than N or greater than N. But we need to see if it can eventually lead to a fixed point.

Let's try to compute the orbit of 72: 72→78 fixed. So 72 works. 216→234 fixed. 648→702 fixed. So it seems that numbers with a=3 and b≥2 work. This is a complicated set.

Given the problem is from an olympiad, there might be a trick: Show that the only possible a1 are those that are multiples of 6 and also are of the form 2*3^b * M where M is a product of primes all >5 and also the exponent of 2 is 1 or 3? But we already have 462=2*3*7*11 works, which is a=1, b=1, M=7*11, and that's a fixed point. So that fits the class a=1, b≥1, and M coprime to 6, 5∤M. So that's one class.

Maybe the only fixed points are those where the three largest proper divisors are exactly N/2, N/3, N/6. That condition is equivalent to: N has at least three proper divisors, and the set of divisors greater than N/6 is exactly {N/2, N/3} (and possibly N itself, but not proper). So we need to characterize all N such that:

1. N is divisible by 6 (so N/2, N/3, N/6 are integers and proper divisors? Actually N/6 is a divisor; N/2 and N/3 are divisors).
2. For any divisor d of N with d > N/6, we have d = N/2 or d = N/3 (or d = N, but not proper).
Thus N/2 and N/3 must be the only divisors greater than N/6. Also note that N/6 itself is a divisor, and we need to ensure that there is no divisor d such that N/6 < d < N/3. That is the condition.

Now, what numbers satisfy that? Let's analyze the divisor structure.

Let N = 2^a 3^b M, with a,b≥1, gcd(M,6)=1. The divisors are 2^i3^j d, with i≤a, j≤b, d|M. The value is > N/6 = 2^{a-1}3^{b-1}M. So we need to consider all triples (i,j,d) such that 2^i3^j d > 2^{a-1}3^{b-1}M. Write the ratio: R = 2^{i-a+1}3^{j-b+1} d / M. Since d|M, d/M ≤ 1. Actually d/M is a rational number. But we can think in terms of the product. The divisors greater than N/6 are those with product > N/6. Let's find the condition for d to be a divisor greater than N/6. For each possible i,j,d, we have 2^i3^j d > 2^{a-1}3^{b-1}M.

Case 1: i=a-1, j=b-1. Then the condition becomes 2^{a-1}3^{b-1} d > 2^{a-1}3^{b-1} M => d > M. Since d|M, the only possibility is d=M? Actually d > M, but d|M implies d ≤ M. So d cannot be >M. Case 2: i=a, j=b-1. Then the condition: 2^a3^{b-1}d > 2^{a-1}3^{b-1}M => 2 d > M => d > M/2. Since d|M, d can be M (if M> M/2) or possibly other divisors? If M has a divisor d > M/2, then d must be M itself because the largest divisor less than M is at most M/2? Actually for any integer M, if d|M and d > M/2, then d=M because the next largest divisor is at most M/2. So the only such d is M. Then we get the divisor N/3 = 2^a3^{b-1}M. So this is the candidate for the second largest.

Case 4: i=a, j=b. Then condition: 2^a3^b d > 2^{a-1}3^{b-1}M => 6 d > M => d > M/6. Since d|M, possible d could be M, or other divisors if they are > M/6. Now, the problem asks: Determine all possible values of a1 such that the infinite sequence exists. The sequence is defined by a_{n+1} = sum of three largest proper divisors of a_n. We need to find all a1 for which there is an infinite sequence (i.e., all terms satisfy the condition of having at least three proper divisors). It might be that the only possibilities are those where the sequence eventually becomes constant at a fixed point, or maybe it could enter a cycle of length >1? We need to analyze the dynamics.

We have computed f(N) for many numbers. Let's see if there are any cycles of length >1. For example, consider numbers that map to themselves (fixed points). Also, some numbers might map to a fixed point after a few steps. For the sequence to be infinite, we need that every term has at least three proper divisors. If at some point we get a number with only two proper divisors (i.e., a prime or a prime square), the sequence would stop because we cannot define the next term (since the condition "each of which has at least three proper divisors" is required for all terms). So we need that the orbit never hits a number with only two proper divisors. So a1 must be such that the orbit stays in S (numbers with at least 3 proper divisors). The dynamics might eventually reach a fixed point, which is fine. But could there be a cycle where none of the terms are primes or prime squares? Let's examine the mapping f(N) for numbers of the form 2^a * 3^b * M with M coprime to 6. We can try to compute f(N) in terms of a,b,M.

We already derived that for N with smallest prime factor 2, the three largest proper divisors are:

- d2 = max( N/3, 2^{a-1}3^{b-1}M? Actually we need to consider the second largest. The divisors greater than N/6? Let's compute the top three proper divisors in general.

Let N = 2^a 3^b M, with M coprime to 6. The divisors are of the form 2^i 3^j d, where i=0..a, j=0..b, d|M. We want the three largest such numbers that are less than N. Since N itself is 2^a3^bM, the largest proper divisor is N/2 = 2^{a-1}3^bM. The second largest: It could be N/3 = 2^a3^{b-1}M, or it could be 2^{a-1}3^b (i.e., N/(2M)?) But N/(2M) = 2^{a-1}3^b, which is not necessarily a divisor? Actually 2^{a-1}3^b is a divisor because i=a-1,j=b,d=1. So it is a divisor. Compare N/3 = 2^a3^{b-1}M and N/(2M)=2^{a-1}3^b. Which is larger? Their ratio is (2^a3^{b-1}M)/(2^{a-1}3^b) = (2M)/3. So N/3 > N/(2M) if 2M > 3. Since M is at least 1, 2M≥2, so if M≥2, then 2M≥4>3, so N/3 > N/(2M). If M=1, then 2M=2<3, so N/3 < N/(2M). But careful: For M=1, N=2^a3^b. Then N/3 = 2^a3^{b-1}, and N/(2M)=2^{a-1}3^b. Which is larger? For a=1,b=2: N=18, N/3=6, N/(2M)=9, so N/(2M) is larger. So the ordering can change. So we need to consider the structure.

Similarly, the third largest proper divisor could be either N/6, or N/(2M), or something else.

Thus, to compute f(N) = d1+d2+d3, we need to determine the top three divisors. This is a combinatorial problem.

Let's try to derive a general expression for f(N). Write N = 2^a * 3^b * M, with M coprime to 6. The divisors are 2^i * 3^j * d, with i=0..a, j=0..b, d|M. We need the three largest such numbers less than N. Since N is 2^a3^bM, the largest proper divisor is N/2 = 2^{a-1}3^bM. Next, we need to consider other divisors.

The second largest could be either N/3 = 2^a3^{b-1}M, or 2^{a-1}3^b (since that's the largest divisor that does not include M? Actually 2^{a-1}3^b is a divisor if a≥1,b≥0. Its value is N/(2M). So compare N/3 and N/(2M). Their ratio is (2M)/3. So if 2M > 3, then N/3 > N/(2M). If 2M = 2 (i.e., M=1), then N/3 = N/3, N/(2M)=N/2, and N/2 > N/3, so second largest is N/2? But N/2 is already the largest? Actually N/2 is the largest proper divisor. So second largest could be N/3 or N/(2M) depending on M. For M=1, N/(2M)=N/2, which is the largest, so not candidate for second. The next candidate is N/3. So for M=1, the top three proper divisors are: d1 = N/2 = 2^{a-1}3^b; d2 = N/3 = 2^a3^{b-1}; d3 = ? It could be N/6 = 2^{a-1}3^{b-1} or maybe 2^{a-2}3^b? Need to compare.

Thus we need to compute the top three divisors in general. This is a classic problem: For a given N, the three largest proper divisors are N/p, N/q, N/r where p,q,r are the three smallest divisors >1? Actually earlier we had: if d2,d3,d4 are the smallest divisors >1 in increasing order, then the three largest proper divisors are N/d2, N/d3, N/d4. So we need to find the three smallest divisors of N greater than 1. Let's denote the divisors of N in ascending order: 1 = d1 < d2 < d3 < ... < d_k = N. Then the largest proper divisors are N/d_{k-1}, N/d_{k-2}, N/d_{k-3}? Actually symmetric: if d_i is a divisor, then N/d_i is also a divisor. The largest proper divisor is N/d_2 (since d_2 is the smallest divisor >1). The second largest proper divisor is N/d_3 (since d_3 is the next smallest divisor). The third largest proper divisor is N/d_4. So f(N) = N/d_2 + N/d_3 + N/d_4. This is a neat formula. So we need to find the three smallest divisors >1 of N.

Thus, the problem reduces to: Given N, let p = d2 (smallest prime factor), q = d3 (next smallest divisor), r = d4 (third smallest divisor). Then f(N) = N/p + N/q + N/r. Note that p is the smallest prime factor. q could be either p^2 (if p^2|N) or the next prime factor if p^2 does not divide N. r could be p^3, p*q, q^2, etc. So we can analyze f(N) by considering the structure.

Now, the sequence a_n must be infinite. So we need that a_n has at least three proper divisors for all n. The set of all numbers that eventually reach a fixed point is exactly the set of numbers that are in the forward orbit of the fixed points under the inverse of f. Thus, the working numbers we have found are:

- All numbers of the form 2*3^b*M with b≥1, M coprime to 6, 5∤M (fixed points).
- All numbers of the form 72*m with m odd and 5∤m (these map to 78*m fixed points).

Are there any numbers that work but are not of these two types? Let's test 2^3*3^2*5=360? 360 has factor 5, fails. 2^3*3^3*5=1080? 1080 has factor 5, fails. So no.

What about numbers with a=1 but M has factor 5? Those are not fixed points, and they may map to something that eventually becomes a fixed point? For example, 30=2*3*5, f=31 prime, fails. 90=2*3^2*5, f=93→35→13, fails. 150=2*3*5^2, f=95→25, fails. 210=2*3*5*7, f=217→? 217=7*31, f=31+7+1=39→? 39=3*13, f=13+3+1=17 prime, fails. So no.

What about numbers with a=3 and b=1? 24 fails. a=3,b=2 works. a=3,b=3 works. a=3,b=4 works. a=3,b=5 fails. So there is a condition on b. But note that for a=3,b=2, we have N=72*m, with m odd and 5∤m. For b=3, N=216*m, with m odd and 5∤m. That's 8*27*m=216m. But does 216m always work? For m=1, 216 works. For m=3, 648 works. For m=5, 1080 fails because 5|1080? Actually 1080=216*5, so m=5 is divisible by 5, so not allowed. For m=7, 1512? 1512=216*7, check if works: 1512=2^3*3^3*7. f=1512/2+1512/3+1512/4=756+504+378=1638, 1638=2*3^2*7*13? Actually 1638/2=819, 819/3=273, 273/3=91, 91=7*13, so 1638=2*3^2*7*13. Then f(1638)=? 1638/2=819, 1638/3=546, 1638/6=273 sum=819+546+273=1638? Wait 819+546=1365, +273=1638, so 1638 is fixed? Check: 1638/2=819, /3=546, /6=273, sum=1638, yes fixed. So 1512 works. So 216m with m odd and 5∤m works. So the class a=3,b≥2 works for any m odd and 5∤m, but note that for b=2, we have N=72m, for b=3, N=216m, etc. So these are numbers with exponent of 2 =3 and exponent of 3 ≥2, and the rest of the factorization is coprime to 6 and not divisible by 5. So N = 8 * 3^b * M, with b≥2, M coprime to 6, 5∤M. That includes 72m, 216m, 648m? Actually 648=8*81=8*3^4, so b=4, works. So b can be any integer ≥2. For b=2,3,4,5? 3^5=243, 8*243=1944, but we saw 1944? Let's test 1944=2^3*3^5=8*243=1944. f=13*1944/12=13*162=2106, 2106=2*3*? 2106/2=1053, 1053=3^4*13, so 2106=2*3*3^4*13=2*3^5*13? Actually 2106=2*3^5*13? 3^5=243, 2*243=486, 486*13=6318? No, 2*3^5*13=2*243*13=6306, not 2106. Let's compute properly: 2106=2*1053=2*3*351=2*3*3*117=2*3^2*117=2*3^2*3*39=2*3^3*39=2*3^3*3*13=2*3^4*13=2*81*13=2106, so 2106=2*3^4*13. Then f(2106)=2106/2+2106/3+2106/6=1053+702+351=2106, fixed? 1053+702=1755, +351=2106, yes fixed. So 1944 works. So b=5 works. So the condition is not on b. For b=2,3,4,5, all work? We had 972=2^3*3^5=8*243=1944? Wait 2^3*3^5=8*243=1944, yes that's b=5. But earlier we said 972 fails? Let's recalc: 972=2^3*3^5? Actually 972/8=121.5, so 972 is not 2^3*3^5. 972=2^2*3^5? 3^5=243, 243*4=972, so exponent of 2 is 2, not 3. So 972 has a=2, not a=3. So our class a=3,b≥2 includes numbers like 2^3*3^2=72, 2^3*3^3=216, 2^3*3^4=648, 2^3*3^5=1944, etc. So 972 is not in that class. So a=3,b=2 works, b=3 works, b=4 works, b=5 works. So all b≥2 work. So the class N = 8 * 3^b * M with b≥2, M coprime to 6, 5∤M works.

Now, could there be numbers with a=1 but M includes a factor 5? Those fail. So the set of working a1 seems to be:

- All numbers N such that N is divisible by 6, and either:
  - N is not divisible by 4 and not divisible by 5 (i.e., N is a fixed point), or
  - N is divisible by 8 and by 3^2? Actually from the preimage class, N must be divisible by 8? Because N=72m=8*9*m, so N divisible by 8. And also N/8 must be divisible by 9? Actually 72m = 8*9*m, so N divisible by 8 and by 9. So N is a multiple of 72. And also N is not divisible by 4? Wait, if N is divisible by 8, it is divisible by 4, so it's not a fixed point. So the preimage class is numbers divisible by 72, and also the rest of the factorization (m) is odd and not divisible by 5. So N = 72 * k, with k odd and 5∤k. That is a subclass of numbers divisible by 8 and 9. So the condition for a1 to work is that a1 is either a fixed point (2||a1 and 5∤a1) or a1 is of the form 72 * k with k odd and 5∤k. But note that fixed points also satisfy 2||a1, which is not divisible by 4, so they are not divisible by 8. So the two classes are disjoint.

Now, are there numbers that are not of these two types but still work? For instance, consider a1=84? No. a1=96? No. a1=108? No. a1=120? No. a1=132? No. a1=144? No. a1=156? No. a1=168? No. a1=180? No. a1=192? No. a1=204? No. a1=210? No. a1=216 works (fixed point? 216 is divisible by 8? 216/8=27, so divisible by 8, but is it a fixed point? No, because 2||216? 216=2^3*3^3, exponent of 2=3, so it is divisible by 4, so it's not a fixed point. But 216 is of the form 72*3=216, and 3 is odd and not divisible by 5, so it belongs to the preimage class. So that's covered.

Now, what about a1=252? 252=2^2*3^2*7, not divisible by 8? 252/8=31.5, so not divisible by 8. Is it a fixed point? Exponent of 2 is 2, so not fixed. So it should fail. And we saw 252 fails? Actually we didn't test 252, but 252 is divisible by 4? 252/4=63, yes. So a=2, b=2? 252=2^2*3^2*7, so a=2, b=2, M=7. We can test 252: divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,7,9,12,14,18,21,28,36,42,63,84,126,252. Smallest >1:2,3,4. f=252/2+252/3+252/4=126+84+63=273. 273=3*7*13, smallest:3,7,13? Actually 273/3=91, 273/7=39, 273/13=21, sum=91+39+21=151, 151 prime? 151 prime, fails. So 252 fails.

Thus, the pattern holds: a1 works if and only if a1 is either a fixed point (i.e., a1 is divisible by 6, not divisible by 4, and not divisible by 5) or a1 is divisible by 72 and the quotient is odd and not divisible by 5. But note that a1 divisible by 72 already implies a1 is divisible by 4, so it's not a fixed point. So the set is:

{ a1 : a1 = 2 * 3^b * M, with b≥1, M coprime to 6, 5∤M } ∪ { a1 : a1 = 72 * k, with k odd, 5∤k }.

But we need to check if the second set is exactly the set of numbers divisible by 72 with the quotient odd and not divisible by 5. <solution>
We were unable to produce a complete proof. However, the strongest partial progress is as follows:
- For an integer N with at least three proper divisors, define f(N) as the sum of its three largest proper divisors.
- A sequence a_n exists iff each a_n has at least three proper divisors, and the sequence is infinite.
- Computational evidence suggests that the only possible a_1 are those that eventually reach a fixed point of f (i.e., f(N)=N). Fixed points are exactly numbers N such that N is divisible by 6, N is not divisible by 4, and N is not divisible by 5. Additionally, numbers that map to such fixed points in one step (like 72k with k odd and not divisible by 5) also work.
- The set of possible a_1 is not fully determined; a rigorous proof would require analyzing the dynamics of f more deeply.

Given the difficulty, we present the partial classification.
</solution>
<self_evaluation>

The problem requires a complete rigorous proof. We have not given a final answer. The partial progress is insufficient for a score of 1 or 0.5; it is essentially a sketch. Since we did not solve the problem, the score should be 0. However, we have attempted to reason about the dynamics, but we did not produce a proof. The self-evaluation should note that the proof is not complete and we have not determined the set of a_1.
